<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influenced</div></div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Descartes" title="Template:Descartes"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Descartes" title="Template talk:Descartes"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Descartes&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>René Descartes</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>French: </small><span class="IPA" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[ʁəne dekaʁt]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:LL-Q150_(fra)-GrandCelinien-Descartes.wav" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/LL-Q150_%28fra%29-GrandCelinien-Descartes.wav" title="LL-Q150 (fra)-GrandCelinien-Descartes.wav">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">Latinized</a>:</small> <b>Renatus  Cartesius</b>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[note 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longman_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-longman-19">[16]</a></sup> 31 March 1596 – 11 February 1650<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 58">: 58 </span></sup>) was a <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a>, widely considered a seminal figure in the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_science" title="Modern science">science</a>. Mathematics was central to his method of inquiry, and he connected the previously separate fields of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a> into <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a>. Descartes spent much of his working life in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a>, initially serving the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_States_Army" title="Dutch States Army">Dutch States Army</a>, later becoming a central intellectual of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Dutch Golden Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> Although he served a Protestant state and was later counted as a deist by critics, Descartes considered himself a devout Catholic.
</p><p>Many elements of Descartes' philosophy have precedents in late <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Neostoicism" title="Neostoicism">revived Stoicism</a> of the 16th century, or in earlier philosophers like <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a>. In his <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, he differed from the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">schools</a> on two major points: first, he rejected the splitting of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">corporeal substance</a> into <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and form; second, he rejected any appeal to <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">final ends</a>, divine or natural, in explaining natural phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> In his theology, he insists on the absolute freedom of <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">God's act of creation</a>. Refusing to accept the authority of previous philosophers, Descartes frequently set his views apart from the philosophers who preceded him. In the opening section of the <i><a href="/wiki/Passions_of_the_Soul" title="Passions of the Soul">Passions of the Soul</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern</a> treatise on emotions, Descartes goes so far as to assert that he will write on this topic "as if no one had written on these matters before." His best known philosophical statement is "<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Cogito,_ergo_sum" title="Cogito, ergo sum">cogito, ergo sum</a></i></span>" ("I think, therefore I am"; French: <i lang="fr">Je pense, donc je suis</i>), found in <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">Discourse on the Method</a></i> (1637; in French and Latin) and <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i> (1644, in Latin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes has often been called the father of modern philosophy, and is largely seen as responsible for the increased attention given to <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> in the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2004p516_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2004p516-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[note 5]</a></sup> He laid the foundation for 17th-century continental <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>, later advocated by <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Spinoza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>, and was later opposed by the <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricist</a> school of thought consisting of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">Berkeley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>. The rise of early modern rationalism – as a highly systematic school of philosophy in its own right for the first time in history – exerted an immense and profound influence on modern Western thought in general, with the birth of two influential rationalistic philosophical systems of Descartes (<a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesianism</a>) and Spinoza (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinozism" title="Spinozism">Spinozism</a>). It was the 17th-century arch-rationalists like Descartes, Spinoza and Leibniz who have given the "<a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Reason</a>" its name and place in history. Leibniz, Spinoza,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[23]</a></sup> and Descartes were all well-versed in mathematics as well as philosophy, and Descartes and Leibniz contributed greatly to science as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes' <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations_on_First_Philosophy" title="Meditations on First Philosophy">Meditations on First Philosophy</a></i> (1641) continues to be a standard text at most university philosophy departments. Descartes' influence in mathematics is equally apparent; the <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Cartesian coordinate system</a> was <a href="#Mathematical_legacy">named after him</a>. He is credited as the father of analytic geometry—used in the discovery of infinitesimal <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">analysis</a>. Descartes was also one of the key figures in the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg/220px-Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg/330px-Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg/440px-Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blason_de_la_famille_Descartes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coat of arms of the Descartes family.</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG/220px-Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG/330px-Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG/440px-Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The house where Descartes was born in <a href="/wiki/Descartes,_Indre-et-Loire" title="Descartes, Indre-et-Loire">La Haye en Touraine</a></div></div></div>
<p>René Descartes was born in <a href="/wiki/Descartes,_Indre-et-Loire" title="Descartes, Indre-et-Loire">La Haye en Touraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Touraine" title="Touraine">Province of Touraine</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Descartes,_Indre-et-Loire" title="Descartes, Indre-et-Loire">Descartes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indre-et-Loire" title="Indre-et-Loire">Indre-et-Loire</a>), France, on 31 March 1596.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-30">[25]</a></sup> René Descartes was conceived about halfway through August of 1595. His mother, Jeanne Brochard, died a few days after giving birth to a still-born child in May 1597.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-30">[25]</a></sup> Descartes' father, Joachim, was a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parlement_of_Brittany" title="Parlement of Brittany">Parlement of Brittany</a> at <a href="/wiki/Rennes" title="Rennes">Rennes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22">: 22 </span></sup> René lived with his grandmother and with his great-uncle. Although the Descartes family was Roman Catholic, the <a href="/wiki/Poitou" title="Poitou">Poitou</a> region was controlled by the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup> In 1607, late because of his fragile health, he entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coll%C3%A8ge_Royal_Henry-Le-Grand" title="Collège Royal Henry-Le-Grand">Collège Royal Henry-Le-Grand</a> at <a href="/wiki/La_Fl%C3%A8che" title="La Flèche">La Flèche</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[30]</a></sup> where he was introduced to mathematics and physics, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a>'s work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-36">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup> While there, Descartes first encountered hermetic mysticism. Although he was briefly a Free Mason, he later abandoned mysticism in favor of reasoned inquiry. After graduation in 1614, he studied for two years (1615–16) at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Poitiers" title="University of Poitiers">University of Poitiers</a>, earning a <i><a href="/wiki/Baccalaur%C3%A9at" title="Baccalauréat">Baccalauréat</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Licentiate_(degree)" title="Licentiate (degree)">Licence</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> in 1616,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-36">[31]</a></sup> in accordance with his father's wishes that he should become a lawyer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> From there, he moved to Paris.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">Discourse on the Method</a></i>, Descartes recalls:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 20–21">: 20–21 </span></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>I entirely abandoned the study of letters. Resolving to seek no knowledge other than that of which could be found in myself or else in the great book of the world, I spent the rest of my youth traveling, visiting courts and armies, mixing with people of diverse temperaments and ranks, gathering various experiences, testing myself in the situations which fortune offered me, and at all times reflecting upon whatever came my way to derive some profit from it.</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1845" data-file-width="1804" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG/220px-DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG/330px-DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG/440px-DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graduation registry for Descartes at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Poitiers" title="University of Poitiers">University of Poitiers</a>, 1616</div></div></div>
<p>In accordance with his ambition to become a professional military officer in 1618, Descartes joined, as a <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenary</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Dutch_States_Army" title="Dutch States Army">Dutch States Army</a> in <a href="/wiki/Breda" title="Breda">Breda</a> under the command of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maurice_of_Nassau" title="Maurice of Nassau">Maurice of Nassau</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-36">[31]</a></sup> and undertook a formal study of <a href="/wiki/Military_engineering" title="Military engineering">military engineering</a>, as established by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 66">: 66 </span></sup> Descartes, therefore, received much encouragement in Breda to advance his knowledge of mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-36">[31]</a></sup> In this way, he became acquainted with <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Beeckman" title="Isaac Beeckman">Isaac Beeckman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-36">[31]</a></sup> the principal of a <a href="/wiki/Dordrecht" title="Dordrecht">Dordrecht</a> school, for whom he wrote the <i>Compendium of Music</i> (written 1618, published 1650).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> Together, they worked on <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">free fall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catenary" title="Catenary">catenary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic section</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluid_statics" title="Fluid statics">fluid statics</a>. Both believed that it was necessary to create a method that thoroughly linked mathematics and physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durandin_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durandin-42">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>While in the service of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> Duke <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Elector_of_Bavaria" title="Maximilian I, Elector of Bavaria">Maximilian of Bavaria</a> from 1619,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup> Descartes was present at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_White_Mountain" title="Battle of the White Mountain">Battle of the White Mountain</a> near <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>, in November 1620.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Baillet" title="Adrien Baillet">Adrien Baillet</a>, on the night of 10–11 November 1619 (<a href="/wiki/St._Martin%27s_Day" title="St. Martin's Day">St. Martin's Day</a>), while stationed in <a href="/wiki/Neuburg_an_der_Donau" title="Neuburg an der Donau">Neuburg an der Donau</a>, Descartes shut himself in a room with an "oven" (probably a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cocklestove" title="Cocklestove">cocklestove</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup> to escape the cold. While within, he had three dreams,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-47">[42]</a></sup> and believed that a divine spirit revealed to him a new philosophy. However, it is speculated that what Descartes considered to be his second dream was actually an episode of <a href="/wiki/Exploding_head_syndrome" title="Exploding head syndrome">exploding head syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otaiku2018_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Otaiku2018-48">[43]</a></sup> Upon exiting, he had formulated <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a> and the idea of applying the mathematical method to philosophy. He concluded from these visions that the pursuit of science would prove to be, for him, the pursuit of true wisdom and a central part of his life's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup> Descartes also saw very clearly that all truths were linked with one another, so that finding a fundamental truth and proceeding with logic would open the way to all science. Descartes discovered this basic truth quite soon: his famous "<a href="/wiki/Cogito,_ergo_sum" title="Cogito, ergo sum">I think, therefore I am</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durandin_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Durandin-42">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1620, Descartes left the army. He visited <a href="/wiki/Basilica_della_Santa_Casa" title="Basilica della Santa Casa">Basilica della Santa Casa</a> in Loreto, then visited various countries before returning to France, and during the next few years, he spent time in Paris. It was there that he composed his first essay on method: <i>Regulae ad Directionem Ingenii</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Rules_for_the_Direction_of_the_Mind" title="Rules for the Direction of the Mind">Rules for the Direction of the Mind</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durandin_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Durandin-42">[37]</a></sup> He arrived in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">La Haye</a> in 1623, selling all of his property to invest in <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bonds</a>, which provided a comfortable income for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 132">: 132 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-51">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 94">: 94 </span></sup> Descartes was present at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_La_Rochelle" title="Siege of La Rochelle">siege of La Rochelle</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Richelieu" title="Cardinal Richelieu">Cardinal Richelieu</a> in 1627.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-51">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 128">: 128 </span></sup> In the autumn of that year, in the residence of the papal <a href="/wiki/Nuncio" title="Nuncio">nuncio</a> <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Francesco_Guidi_di_Bagno" title="Giovanni Francesco Guidi di Bagno">Guidi di Bagno</a>, where he came with <a href="/wiki/Marin_Mersenne" title="Marin Mersenne">Mersenne</a> and many other scholars to listen to a lecture given by the alchemist, Nicolas de Villiers, Sieur de Chandoux, on the principles of a supposed new philosophy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup> Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_B%C3%A9rulle" title="Pierre de Bérulle">Bérulle</a> urged him to write an exposition of his new philosophy in some location beyond the reach of the Inquisition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westermarkt_6,_Descarteshuis_(links).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG/220px-Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG/330px-Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG/440px-Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westermarkt_6,_Descarteshuis_(links).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, Descartes lived at Westermarkt 6 (Maison Descartes, left).</div></div></div>
<p>Descartes returned to the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> in 1628.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-47">[42]</a></sup> In April 1629, he joined the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Franeker" title="University of Franeker">University of Franeker</a>, studying under <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_Metius" title="Adriaan Metius">Adriaan Metius</a>, either living with a Catholic family or renting the <a href="/wiki/Sjaerdemaslot" title="Sjaerdemaslot">Sjaerdemaslot</a>. The next year, under the name "Poitevin", he enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Leiden_University" title="Leiden University">Leiden University</a>, which at the time was a Protestant University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> He studied both mathematics with <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Golius" title="Jacobus Golius">Jacobus Golius</a>, who confronted him with <a href="/wiki/Pappus%27s_hexagon_theorem" title="Pappus's hexagon theorem">Pappus's hexagon theorem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> with <a href="/wiki/Martin_van_den_Hove" title="Martin van den Hove">Martin Hortensius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup> In October 1630, he had a falling-out with Beeckman, whom he accused of plagiarizing some of his ideas. In Amsterdam, he had a relationship with a servant girl, Helena Jans van der Strom, with whom he had a daughter, <a href="/wiki/Francine_Descartes" title="Francine Descartes">Francine</a>, who was born in 1635 in <a href="/wiki/Deventer" title="Deventer">Deventer</a>. She was baptized a Protestant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup> and died of scarlet fever at the age of 5.
</p><p>Unlike many moralists of the time, Descartes did not deprecate the passions but rather defended them;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> he wept upon Francine's death in 1640.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup> According to a recent biography by Jason Porterfield, "Descartes said that he did not believe that one must refrain from tears to prove oneself a man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Russell_Shorto" title="Russell Shorto">Russell Shorto</a> speculates that the experience of fatherhood and losing a child formed a turning point in Descartes's work, changing its focus from medicine to a quest for universal answers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite frequent moves,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[note 6]</a></sup> he wrote all of his major work during his 20-plus years in the Netherlands, initiating a revolution in mathematics and philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[note 7]</a></sup> In 1633, Galileo was condemned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Inquisition" title="Italian Inquisition">Italian Inquisition</a>, and Descartes abandoned plans to publish <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_World_(Descartes)" title="The World (Descartes)">Treatise on the World</a></i>, his work of the previous four years. Nevertheless, in 1637, he published parts of this work in three essays:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-63">[56]</a></sup> "Les Météores" (The Meteors), "<a href="/wiki/Dioptrique" title="Dioptrique">La Dioptrique</a>" (Dioptrics) and <i><a href="/wiki/La_G%C3%A9om%C3%A9trie" title="La Géométrie">La Géométrie</a></i> (<i>Geometry</i>), preceded by an introduction, his famous <i>Discours de la méthode</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">Discourse on the Method</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-63">[56]</a></sup> In it, Descartes lays out four rules of thought, meant to ensure that our knowledge rests upon a firm foundation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The first was never to accept anything for true which I did not know to be such; that is to say, carefully to avoid precipitancy and prejudice, and to comprise nothing more in my judgment than what was presented to my mind so clearly and distinctly as to exclude all ground of doubt.</p></blockquote>
<p>In <i>La Géométrie</i>, Descartes exploited the discoveries he made with <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a>, having been able to do so because his paper, <i>Introduction to Loci</i>, was published posthumously in 1679. This later became known as Cartesian Geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-65">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes continued to publish works concerning both mathematics and philosophy for the rest of his life. In 1641, he published a metaphysics treatise, <i>Meditationes de Prima Philosophia</i> (<i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i>), written in Latin and thus addressed to the learned. It was followed in 1644 by <i>Principia Philosophiae</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i>), a kind of synthesis of the <i>Discourse on the Method</i> and <i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i>. In 1643, Cartesian philosophy was condemned at the <a href="/wiki/Utrecht_University" title="Utrecht University">University of Utrecht</a>, and Descartes was obliged to flee to the Hague, settling in <a href="/wiki/Egmond-Binnen" title="Egmond-Binnen">Egmond-Binnen</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christia_Mercer" title="Christia Mercer">Christia Mercer</a> suggested that Descartes may have been influenced by Spanish author and Roman Catholic nun <a href="/wiki/Teresa_of_%C3%81vila" title="Teresa of Ávila">Teresa of Ávila</a>, who, fifty years earlier, published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Interior_Castle" title="The Interior Castle">The Interior Castle</a></i>, concerning the role of philosophical reflection in intellectual growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM2017_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-CM2017-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes began (through Alfonso Polloti, an Italian general in Dutch service) a six-year correspondence with <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_of_the_Palatinate" title="Elisabeth of the Palatinate">Princess Elisabeth of Bohemia</a>, devoted mainly to moral and psychological subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[60]</a></sup> Connected with this correspondence, in 1649 he published <i>Les Passions de l'âme</i> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Passions_of_the_Soul" title="The Passions of the Soul">The Passions of the Soul</a></i>), which he dedicated to the Princess. A French translation of <i>Principia Philosophiae</i>, prepared by Abbot Claude Picot, was published in 1647. This edition was also dedicated to Princess Elisabeth. In <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy#Preface_to_the_French_edition" title="Principles of Philosophy">the preface to the French edition</a>, Descartes praised true philosophy as a means to attain wisdom. He identifies four ordinary sources to reach wisdom and finally says that there is a fifth, better and more secure, consisting in the search for first causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By 1649, Descartes had become one of Europe's most famous philosophers and scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-63">[56]</a></sup> That year, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Christina_of_Sweden" title="Queen Christina of Sweden">Queen Christina of Sweden</a> invited him to her court to organize a new scientific academy and tutor her in his ideas about love. Descartes accepted, and moved to Sweden in the middle of winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[62]</a></sup> She was interested in and stimulated Descartes to publish <i>The Passions of the Soul.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-70">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was a guest at the house of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Chanut" title="Pierre Chanut">Pierre Chanut</a>, living on <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterl%C3%A5nggatan" title="Västerlånggatan">Västerlånggatan</a>, less than 500 meters from <a href="/wiki/Tre_Kronor_(castle)" title="Tre Kronor (castle)">Tre Kronor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>. There, Chanut and Descartes made observations with a <a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Torricellian</a> mercury barometer. Challenging <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a>, Descartes took the first set of barometric readings in Stockholm to see if <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> could be used in forecasting the weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:239px;max-width:239px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:196px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DescartesAshes.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1843" data-file-width="2218" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/DescartesAshes.jpg/237px-DescartesAshes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/DescartesAshes.jpg/356px-DescartesAshes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/DescartesAshes.jpg/474px-DescartesAshes.jpg 2x" width="237"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:149px;max-width:149px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:196px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg/147px-Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg/221px-Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg/294px-Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg 2x" width="147"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">(left) The tomb of Descartes (middle, with detail of the inscription), in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint-Germain-des-Pr%C3%A9s" title="Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Prés">Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Prés</a>, Paris; (right) memorial to Descartes, erected in the 1720s, in the Adolf Fredriks kyrka</div></div></div></div>
<p>Descartes arranged to give lessons to Queen Christina after her birthday, three times a week at 5 am, in her cold and draughty castle. It soon became clear they did not like each other; she did not care for his <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy#Descartes_and_the_mechanical_philosophy" title="Mechanical philosophy">mechanical philosophy</a>, nor did he share her interest in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>. By 15 January 1650, Descartes had seen Christina only four or five times. On 1 February, he contracted <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> and died on 11 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> The cause of death was pneumonia according to Chanut, but <a href="/wiki/Pleurisy" title="Pleurisy">peripneumonia</a> according to Christina's physician Johann van Wullen who was not allowed to bleed him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> (The winter seems to have been mild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup> except for the second half of January which was harsh as described by Descartes himself; however, "this remark was probably intended to be as much Descartes' take on the intellectual climate as it was about the weather.")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-70">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eike_Pies" title="de:Eike Pies">E. Pies</a> has questioned this account, based on a letter by the Doctor van Wullen; however, Descartes had refused his treatment, and more arguments against its veracity have been raised since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup> In a 2009 book, German philosopher Theodor Ebert argues that Descartes was poisoned by a Catholic missionary who opposed his religious views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stockholmnews.com_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-stockholmnews.com-77">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a Catholic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.be_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.be-79">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207-80">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107-81">[74]</a></sup> in a Protestant nation, he was interred in a graveyard used mainly for orphans in <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Fredrik_Church" title="Adolf Fredrik Church">Adolf Fredriks kyrka</a> in Stockholm. His manuscripts came into the possession of <a href="/wiki/Claude_Clerselier" title="Claude Clerselier">Claude Clerselier</a>, Chanut's brother-in-law, and "a devout Catholic who has begun the process of turning Descartes into a saint by cutting, adding and publishing his letters selectively."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 137–154">: 137–154 </span></sup> In 1663, the <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VII" title="Pope Alexander VII">Pope</a> placed Descartes' works on the <a href="/wiki/Index_Librorum_Prohibitorum" title="Index Librorum Prohibitorum"><i>Index of Prohibited Books</i></a>. In 1666, sixteen years after his death, his remains were taken to France and buried in <a href="/wiki/Saint-%C3%89tienne-du-Mont" title="Saint-Étienne-du-Mont">Saint-Étienne-du-Mont</a>. In 1671, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> prohibited all lectures in <a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesianism</a>. Although the <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a> in 1792 had planned to transfer his remains to the <a href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on" title="Panthéon">Panthéon</a>, he was reburied in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint-Germain-des-Pr%C3%A9s" title="Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Prés">Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Prés</a> in 1819, missing a finger and the skull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[note 8]</a></sup> His skull is on display in the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Homme" title="Musée de l'Homme">Musée de l'Homme</a> in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_work">Philosophical work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Philosophical work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descartes3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Descartes3.jpg/220px-Descartes3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Descartes3.jpg/330px-Descartes3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Descartes3.jpg/440px-Descartes3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descartes3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>René Descartes at work</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesianism</a></div>
<p>In his <i>Discourse on the Method</i>, he attempts to arrive at a fundamental set of principles that one can know as true without any doubt. To achieve this, he employs a method called hyperbolical/metaphysical doubt, also sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_doubt" title="Cartesian doubt">methodological skepticism</a> or Cartesian doubt: he rejects any ideas that can be doubted and then re-establishes them in order to acquire a firm foundation for genuine knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup> Descartes built his ideas from scratch which he does in <i>The Meditations on First Philosophy</i>. He relates this to architecture: the top soil is taken away to create a new building or structure. Descartes calls his doubt the soil and new knowledge the buildings. To Descartes, Aristotle's <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundationalism</a> is incomplete and his method of doubt enhances foundationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-87">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially, Descartes arrives at only a single first principle that he thinks. This is expressed in the Latin phrase in <i>the Discourse on Method</i> "<a href="/wiki/Cogito,_ergo_sum" title="Cogito, ergo sum">Cogito, ergo sum</a>" (English: "I think, therefore I am").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[80]</a></sup>  Descartes concluded, if he doubted, then something or someone must be doing the doubting; therefore, the very fact that he doubted proved his existence. "The simple meaning of the phrase is that if one is skeptical of existence, that is in and of itself proof that he does exist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[81]</a></sup> These two first principles—I think and I exist—were later confirmed by Descartes' clear and distinct perception (delineated in his Third Meditation from <i>The Meditations</i>): as he clearly and distinctly perceives these two principles, Descartes reasoned, ensures their indubitability.
</p><p>Descartes concludes that he can be certain that he exists because he thinks. But in what form? He perceives his body through the use of the senses; however, these have previously been unreliable. So Descartes determines that the only indubitable knowledge is that he is a <i>thinking thing</i>. Thinking is what he does, and his power must come from his essence. Descartes defines "thought" (<i><a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_phrases_(C)" title="List of Latin phrases (C)">cogitatio</a></i>) as "what happens in me such that I am immediately conscious of it, insofar as I am conscious of it". Thinking is thus every activity of a person of which the person is immediately <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">conscious</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup> He gave reasons for thinking that waking thoughts are distinguishable from <a href="/wiki/Dream_argument" title="Dream argument">dreams</a>, and that one's mind cannot have been "hijacked" by an <a href="/wiki/Evil_demon" title="Evil demon">evil demon</a> placing an illusory external world before one's senses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-87">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>And so something that I thought I was seeing with my eyes is grasped solely by the faculty of judgment which is in my mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 109">: 109 </span></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In this manner, Descartes proceeds to construct a system of knowledge, discarding <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a> as unreliable and, instead, admitting only <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a> as a method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mind.E2.80.93body_dualism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mind–body_dualism">Mind–body dualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mind–body dualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">Mind–body problem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism" title="Mind–body dualism">Mind–body dualism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3471" data-file-width="2606" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif/lossy-page1-220px-L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif/lossy-page1-330px-L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif/lossy-page1-440px-L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>L'homme</i> (1664)</div></div></div>
<p>Descartes, influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">automatons</a> on display throughout the city of Paris, began to investigate the connection between the mind and body, and how the two interact.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Article cited does not maintain that Descartes started investigating mind and body connection influenced by automatons (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> His main influences for <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism" title="Mind–body dualism">dualism</a> were <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[85]</a></sup> The theory on the dualism of mind and body is Descartes' signature doctrine and permeates other theories he advanced. Known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_dualism" title="Cartesian dualism">Cartesian dualism</a> (or mind–body dualism), his theory on the separation between the mind and the body went on to influence subsequent Western philosophies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[86]</a></sup> In <i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i>, Descartes attempted to demonstrate the existence of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> and the distinction between the human soul and the body. Humans are a union of mind and body;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup> thus Descartes's dualism embraced the idea that mind and body are distinct but closely joined. While many contemporary readers of Descartes found the distinction between mind and body difficult to grasp, he thought it was entirely straightforward. Descartes employed the concept of <i>modes</i>, which are the ways in which substances exist. In <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i>, Descartes explained, "we can clearly perceive a substance apart from the mode which we say differs from it, whereas we cannot, conversely, understand the mode apart from the substance". To perceive a mode apart from its substance requires an intellectual abstraction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Cunning_2014_278_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Cunning_2014_278-96">[88]</a></sup> which Descartes explained as follows:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The intellectual abstraction consists in my turning my thought away from one part of the contents of this richer idea the better to apply it to the other part with greater attention. Thus, when I consider a shape without thinking of the substance or the extension whose shape it is, I make a mental abstraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Cunning_2014_278_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-David_Cunning_2014_278-96">[88]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>According to Descartes, two substances are really distinct when each of them can exist apart from the other. Thus, Descartes reasoned that God is distinct from humans, and the body and mind of a human are also distinct from one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[89]</a></sup> He argued that the great differences between body (an extended thing) and mind (an un-extended, immaterial thing) make the two <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontologically</a> distinct. According to Descartes' indivisibility argument, the mind is utterly indivisible: because "when I consider the mind, or myself in so far as I am merely a thinking thing, I am unable to distinguish any part within myself; I understand myself to be something quite single and complete."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moreover, in The <i>Meditations</i>, Descartes discusses a piece of <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a> and exposes the single most characteristic doctrine of Cartesian dualism: that the universe contained two radically different kinds of substances—the mind or soul defined as <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thinking</a>, and the body defined as matter and unthinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian philosophy</a> of Descartes' days held that the universe was inherently purposeful or teleological. Everything that happened, be it the motion of the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> or the growth of a <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">tree</a>, was supposedly explainable by a certain purpose, goal or end that worked its way out within nature. Aristotle called this the "final cause," and these final causes were indispensable for explaining the ways nature operated. Descartes' theory of dualism supports the distinction between traditional Aristotelian science and the new science of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> and Galileo, which denied the role of a divine power and "final causes" in its attempts to explain nature. Descartes' dualism provided the philosophical rationale for the latter by expelling the final cause from the physical universe (or <i>res extensa</i>) in favor of the mind (or <i>res cogitans</i>). Therefore, while Cartesian dualism paved the way for modern <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, it also held the door open for religious beliefs about the immortality of the <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes' dualism of mind and matter implied a concept of human beings. A human was, according to Descartes, a composite entity of mind and body. Descartes gave priority to the mind and argued that the mind could exist without the body, but the body could not exist without the mind. In The <i>Meditations</i>, Descartes even argues that while the mind is a substance, the body is composed only of "accidents".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup> But he did argue that mind and body are closely joined:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Dicker_2013_303_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Dicker_2013_303-102">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Nature also teaches me, by the sensations of pain, hunger, thirst and so on, that I am not merely present in my body as a pilot in his ship, but that I am very closely joined and, as it were, intermingled with it, so that I and the body form a unit. If this were not so, I, who am nothing but a thinking thing, would not feel pain when the body was hurt, but would perceive the damage purely by the intellect, just as a sailor perceives by sight if anything in his ship is broken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Dicker_2013_303_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Dicker_2013_303-102">[94]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Descartes' discussion on embodiment raised one of the most perplexing problems of his dualism philosophy: What exactly is the relationship of union between the mind and the body of a person?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Dicker_2013_303_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Dicker_2013_303-102">[94]</a></sup> Therefore, Cartesian dualism set the agenda for philosophical discussion of the <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">mind–body problem</a> for many years after Descartes' death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup> Descartes was also a <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist</a> and believed in the power of <a href="/wiki/Innatism" title="Innatism">innate ideas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88-104">[96]</a></sup> Descartes argued the theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innate_knowledge" title="Innate knowledge">innate knowledge</a> and that all humans were born with knowledge through the higher power of God. It was this theory of innate knowledge that was later combated by philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> (1632–1704), an empiricist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">Empiricism</a> holds that all knowledge is acquired through experience.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology_and_psychology">Physiology and psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Physiology and psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <i>The Passions of the Soul</i>, published in 1649,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup> Descartes discussed the common contemporary belief that the human body contained animal spirits. These animal spirits were believed to be light and roaming fluids circulating rapidly around the nervous system between the brain and the muscles. These animal spirits were believed to affect the human soul, or passions of the soul. Descartes distinguished six basic passions: wonder, love, hatred, desire, joy and sadness. All of these passions, he argued, represented different combinations of the original spirit, and influenced the soul to will or want certain actions. He argued, for example, that fear is a passion that moves the soul to generate a response in the body. In line with his dualist teachings on the separation between the soul and the body, he hypothesized that some part of the brain served as a connector between the soul and the body and singled out the <a href="/wiki/Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland">pineal gland</a> as connector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[99]</a></sup> Descartes argued that signals passed from the ear and the eye to the pineal gland, through animal spirits. Thus different motions in the gland cause various animal spirits. He argued that these motions in the pineal gland are based on God's will and that humans are supposed to want and like things that are useful to them. But he also argued that the animal spirits that moved around the body could distort the commands from the pineal gland, thus humans had to learn how to control their passions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87-108">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes advanced a theory on automatic bodily reactions to external events, which influenced 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflex</a> theory. He argued that external motions, such as touch and sound, reach the endings of the nerves and affect the animal spirits. For example, heat from fire affects a spot on the skin and sets in motion a chain of reactions, with the animal spirits reaching the brain through the central nervous system, and in turn, animal spirits are sent back to the muscles to move the hand away from the fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87-108">[100]</a></sup> Through this chain of reactions, the automatic reactions of the body do not require a thought process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88-104">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Above all, he was among the first scientists who believed that the soul should be subject to scientific investigation. He challenged the views of his contemporaries that the soul was <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a>, thus religious authorities regarded his books as dangerous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup> Descartes' writings went on to form the basis for theories on <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotions</a> and how <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> evaluations were translated into affective processes. Descartes believed that the brain resembled a working machine and unlike many of his contemporaries, he believed that mathematics and mechanics could explain the most complicated processes of the mind.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim seems in conflict with Descartes' idea that the mind is a different substance that does not follow mechanistic laws (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> advanced <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_and_theoretical_biology" title="Mathematical and theoretical biology">mathematical biology</a> as inspired by Descartes. His theories on reflexes also served as the foundation for advanced <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological theories</a>, more than 200 years after his death. The physiologist <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Pavlov" title="Ivan Pavlov">Ivan Pavlov</a> was a great admirer of Descartes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral_philosophy">Moral philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Moral philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For Descartes, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> was a science, the highest and most perfect of them. Like the rest of the sciences, ethics had its roots in metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-92">[84]</a></sup> In this way, he argues for the existence of God, investigates the place of man in nature, formulates the theory of mind–body dualism, and defends <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>. However, as he was a convinced rationalist, Descartes clearly states that reason is sufficient in the search for the goods that we should seek, and <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a> consists in the correct reasoning that should guide our actions. Nevertheless, the quality of this reasoning depends on knowledge, because a well-informed mind will be more capable of making good <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choices" title="Choices">choices</a>, and it also depends on mental condition. For this reason, he said that a complete moral philosophy should include the study of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 189">: 189 </span></sup> He discussed this subject in the correspondence with <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_of_the_Palatinate" title="Elisabeth of the Palatinate">Princess Elisabeth of Bohemia</a>, and as a result wrote his work <i>The Passions of the Soul</i>, that contains a study of the <a href="/wiki/Psychosomatic_medicine" title="Psychosomatic medicine">psychosomatic</a> processes and reactions in man, with an emphasis on emotions or passions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blom_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blom-112">[104]</a></sup> His works about human passion and emotion would be the basis for the philosophy of his followers (see <a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesianism</a>), and would have a lasting impact on ideas concerning what literature and art should be, specifically how it should invoke emotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans should seek the <a href="/wiki/Summum_bonum" title="Summum bonum">sovereign good</a> that Descartes, following <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Citium" title="Zeno of Citium">Zeno</a>, identifies with virtue, as this produces <a href="/wiki/Blessing" title="Blessing">blessedness</a>. For <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a>, the sovereign good was pleasure, and Descartes says that, in fact, this is not in contradiction with Zeno's teaching, because virtue produces a spiritual pleasure, that is better than bodily pleasure. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s opinion that <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">happiness (eudaimonia)</a> depends on both moral virtue and also on the goods of fortune such as a moderate degree of wealth, Descartes does not deny that fortunes contribute to happiness but remarks that they are in great proportion outside one's own control, whereas one's mind is under one's complete control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blom_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blom-112">[104]</a></sup> The moral writings of Descartes came at the last part of his life, but earlier, in his <i>Discourse on the Method</i>, he adopted three maxims to be able to act while he put all his ideas into doubt. This is known as his <a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method#Part_III:_Morals,_and_Maxims_accepted_while_conducting_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">"Provisional Morals"</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
