</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism portal</a></li><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template talk:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Catholic_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the third and fifth <i>Meditation</i>, Descartes offers <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof (truth)">proofs</a> of a benevolent God (the <a href="/wiki/Trademark_argument" title="Trademark argument">trademark argument</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ontological_argument" title="Ontological argument">ontological argument</a> respectively). Because God is benevolent, Descartes has faith in the account of reality his senses provide him, for God has provided him with a working mind and <a href="/wiki/Sensory_nervous_system" title="Sensory nervous system">sensory system</a> and does not desire to deceive him. From this supposition, however, Descartes finally establishes the possibility of acquiring knowledge about the world based on deduction and perception. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, therefore, Descartes can be said to have contributed such ideas as a rigorous conception of foundationalism and the possibility that <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> is the only reliable method of attaining knowledge. Descartes, however, was very much aware that experimentation was necessary to verify and validate theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes invokes his <a href="/wiki/Causal_adequacy_principle" title="Causal adequacy principle">causal adequacy principle</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup> to support his trademark argument for the existence of God, quoting Lucretius in defence: <i>"Ex nihilo nihil fit"</i>, meaning "<a href="/wiki/Nothing_comes_from_nothing" title="Nothing comes from nothing">Nothing comes from nothing</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[107]</a></sup> <i>Oxford Reference</i> summarises the argument, as follows, "that our idea of perfection is related to its perfect origin (God), just as a stamp or trademark is left in an article of workmanship by its maker."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[108]</a></sup> In the fifth Meditation, Descartes presents a version of the ontological argument which is founded on the possibility of thinking the "idea of a being that is supremely perfect and infinite," and suggests that "of all the ideas that are in me, the idea that I have of God is the most true, the most clear and distinct."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes considered himself to be a devout Catholic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.be_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.be-79">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207-80">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107-81">[74]</a></sup> and one of the purposes of the <i>Meditations</i> was to defend the Catholic faith. His attempt to ground theological beliefs on reason encountered intense opposition in his time. <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Pascal</a> regarded Descartes' views as a rationalist and mechanist, and accused him of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>: "I cannot forgive Descartes; in all his philosophy, Descartes did his best to dispense with God. But Descartes could not avoid prodding God to set the world in motion with a snap of his lordly fingers; after that, he had no more use for God," while a powerful contemporary, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Schoock" title="Martin Schoock">Martin Schoock</a>, accused him of <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a> beliefs, though Descartes had provided an explicit critique of atheism in his <i>Meditations</i>. The Catholic Church prohibited his books in 1663.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes,_René_2009_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes,_René_2009-118">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 274">: 274 </span></sup>
</p><p>Descartes also wrote a response to <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">external world skepticism</a>. Through this method of skepticism, he does not doubt for the sake of doubting but to achieve concrete and reliable information. In other words, certainty. He argues that sensory <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perceptions</a> come to him involuntarily, and are not willed by him. They are external to his senses, and according to Descartes, this is evidence of the existence of something outside of his mind, and thus, an external world. Descartes goes on to show that the things in the external world are material by arguing that God would not deceive him as to the ideas that are being transmitted, and that God has given him the "propensity" to believe that such ideas are caused by material things. Descartes also believes a substance is something that does not need any assistance to function or exist. Descartes further explains how only God can be a true "substance". But minds are substances, meaning they need only God for it to function. The mind is a thinking substance. The means for a thinking substance stem from ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-121">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes steered clear of theological questions, restricting his attention to showing that there is no incompatibility between his metaphysics and theological orthodoxy. He avoided trying to demonstrate theological dogmas metaphysically. When challenged that he had not established the immortality of the soul merely in showing that the soul and the body are distinct substances, he replied, "I do not take it upon myself to try to use the power of human reason to settle any of those matters which depend on the free will of God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_science">Natural science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Natural science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Descartes is often regarded as the first thinker to emphasize the use of reason to develop the <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup> For him, philosophy was a thinking system that embodied all knowledge, as he related in a letter to a French translator:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Thus, all Philosophy is like a tree, of which Metaphysics is the root, Physics the trunk, and all the other sciences the branches that grow out of this trunk, which are reduced to three principals, namely, Medicine, Mechanics, and Ethics. By the science of Morals, I understand the highest and most perfect which, presupposing an entire knowledge of the other sciences, is the last degree of wisdom.</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_animals">On animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: On animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Descartes denied that animals had reason or intelligence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[116]</a></sup> He argued that animals did not lack sensations or perceptions, but these could be explained mechanistically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup> Whereas humans had a soul, or mind, and were able to feel <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, animals by virtue of not having a soul could not feel pain or anxiety. If animals showed signs of distress then this was to protect the body from damage, but the innate state needed for them to <a href="/wiki/Suffering" title="Suffering">suffer</a> was absent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup> Although Descartes' views were not universally accepted, they became prominent in Europe and North America, allowing humans to treat animals with impunity. The view that animals were quite separate from humanity and merely <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machines</a> allowed for the <a href="/wiki/Cruelty_to_animals" title="Cruelty to animals">maltreatment of animals</a>, and was sanctioned in law and societal norms until the middle of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 180–214">: 180–214 </span></sup> The publications of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> would eventually erode the Cartesian view of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 37">: 37 </span></sup> Darwin argued that the continuity between humans and other species opened the possibilities that animals did not have dissimilar properties to suffer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 177">: 177 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_impact">Historical impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Historical impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emancipation_from_Church_doctrine">Emancipation from Church doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Emancipation from Church doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DescartesMeditations.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="345" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/DescartesMeditations.png/220px-DescartesMeditations.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/DescartesMeditations.png/330px-DescartesMeditations.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/DescartesMeditations.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DescartesMeditations.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of <i>Meditations</i></div></div></div>
<p>Descartes has often been dubbed the father of modern <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a>, the thinker whose approach has profoundly changed the course of Western philosophy and set the basis for <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2004p516_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2004p516-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup> The first two of his <i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i>, those that formulate the famous methodic doubt, represent the portion of Descartes's writings that most influenced modern thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup> It has been argued that Descartes himself did not realize the extent of this revolutionary move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[124]</a></sup> In shifting the debate from "what is true" to "of what can I be certain?", Descartes arguably shifted the authoritative guarantor of truth from God to humanity (even though Descartes himself claimed he received his visions from God)—while the traditional concept of "truth" implies an external authority, "certainty" instead relies on the judgment of the individual.
</p><p>In an <a href="/wiki/Anthropocentrism" title="Anthropocentrism">anthropocentric</a> revolution, the human being is now raised to the level of a subject, an agent, an <a href="/wiki/Emancipation" title="Emancipation">emancipated</a> being equipped with autonomous reason. This was a revolutionary step that established the basis of modernity, the repercussions of which are still being felt: the emancipation of humanity from Christian <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelational</a> truth and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Church doctrine</a>; humanity making its own law and taking its own stand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heidegger1938emancipated_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heidegger1938emancipated-133">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ingraffia95p126_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ingraffia95p126-134">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[127]</a></sup> In modernity, the guarantor of truth is not God anymore but human beings, each of whom is a "self-conscious shaper and guarantor" of their own reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovitt77pxxv_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovitt77pxxv-136">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[129]</a></sup> In that way, each person is turned into a reasoning adult, a subject and agent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovitt77pxxv_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lovitt77pxxv-136">[128]</a></sup> as opposed to a child obedient to God. This change in perspective was characteristic of the shift from the Christian medieval period to the modern period, a shift that had been anticipated in other fields, and which was now being formulated in the field of philosophy by Descartes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovitt77pxxv_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lovitt77pxxv-136">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>This anthropocentric perspective of Descartes's work, establishing human reason as autonomous, provided the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>'s emancipation from God and the Church. According to <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a>, the perspective of Descartes's work also provided the basis for all subsequent <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heidegger19382002p75_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heidegger19382002p75-139">[131]</a></sup> Descartes's philosophical revolution is sometimes said to have sparked modern <a href="/wiki/Anthropocentrism" title="Anthropocentrism">anthropocentrism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subjectivism" title="Subjectivism">subjectivism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2004p516_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2004p516-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_legacy">Mathematical legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mathematical legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="742" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg/220px-Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg/330px-Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg/440px-Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cartesian_coordinates_2D.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Cartesian coordinates graph, using his invented <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> axes</div></div></div>
<p>One of Descartes's most enduring legacies was his development of Cartesian or analytic geometry, which uses algebra to describe geometry. Descartes "invented the convention of representing unknowns in equations by <i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, and <i>z</i>, and knowns by <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i>". He also "pioneered the standard notation" that uses <a href="/wiki/Subscript_and_superscript" title="Subscript and superscript">superscripts</a> to show the powers or exponents; for example, the 2 used in x<sup>2</sup> to indicate x squared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 19">: 19 </span></sup> He was first to assign a fundamental place for algebra in the system of knowledge, using it as a method to automate or mechanize reasoning, particularly about abstract, unknown quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 91–114">: 91–114 </span></sup> European mathematicians had previously viewed geometry as a more fundamental form of mathematics, serving as the foundation of algebra. Algebraic rules were given geometric proofs by mathematicians such as <a href="/wiki/Luca_Pacioli" title="Luca Pacioli">Pacioli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerolamo_Cardano" title="Gerolamo Cardano">Cardan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Fontana_Tartaglia" title="Niccolò Fontana Tartaglia">Tartaglia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lodovico_Ferrari" title="Lodovico Ferrari">Ferrari</a>. Equations of <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_a_polynomial" title="Degree of a polynomial">degree</a> higher than the third were regarded as unreal, because a three-dimensional form, such as a cube, occupied the largest dimension of reality. Descartes professed that the abstract quantity <i>a<sup>2</sup></i> could represent length as well as an area. This was in opposition to the teachings of mathematicians such as <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Vi%C3%A8te" title="François Viète">François Viète</a>, who insisted that a second power must represent an area. Although Descartes did not pursue the subject, he preceded <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz</a> in envisioning a more general science of algebra or "universal mathematics," as a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">symbolic logic</a>, that could encompass logical principles and methods symbolically, and mechanize general reasoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 280–281">: 280–281 </span></sup>
</p><p>Descartes's work provided the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a> and Leibniz, who applied the infinitesimal calculus to the <a href="/wiki/Tangent#Analytical_approach" title="Tangent">tangent line problem</a>, thus permitting the evolution of that branch of modern mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Descartes%27_rule_of_signs" title="Descartes' rule of signs">rule of signs</a> is also a commonly used method to determine the number of positive and negative roots of a polynomial.
</p><p>The beginning to Descartes's interest in physics is accredited to the amateur scientist and mathematician Isaac Beeckman, who was at the forefront of a new school of thought known as <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy" title="Mechanical philosophy">mechanical philosophy</a>. With this foundation of reasoning, Descartes formulated many of his theories on <a href="/wiki/Geometric_mechanics" title="Geometric mechanics">mechanical and geometric physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slowik_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slowik-148">[140]</a></sup> Descartes discovered an early form of the law of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">conservation of momentum</a> (a measure of the motion of an object), and envisioned it as pertaining to motion in a straight line, as opposed to perfect circular motion, as Galileo had envisioned it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slowik_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Slowik-148">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup> He outlined his views on the universe in his <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i>, where he describes his three <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_motion" title="Laws of motion">laws of motion</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Disambiguation/Fixing_links" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Disambiguation/Fixing links"><span title="Link needs disambiguation (August 2022)">disambiguation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[142]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's own laws of motion</a> would later be modeled on Descartes' exposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slowik_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-Slowik-148">[140]</a></sup>)
</p><p>Descartes also made contributions to the field of <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>. He showed by using geometric construction and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_refraction" title="Law of refraction">law of refraction</a> (also known as Descartes's law, or more commonly <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell's law">Snell's law</a> outside France) that the angular radius of a <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbow</a> is 42 degrees (i.e., the angle subtended at the eye by the edge of the rainbow and the ray passing from the sun through the rainbow's centre is 42°).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[143]</a></sup> He also independently discovered the <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">law of reflection</a>, and his essay on optics was the first published mention of this law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Influence_on_Newton.27s_mathematics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_Newton's_mathematics">Influence on Newton's mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Influence on Newton's mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Current popular opinion holds that Descartes had the most influence of anyone on the young <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, and this is arguably one of his most important contributions. Decartes' influence extended not directly from his original French edition of <i>La Géométrie</i>, however, but rather from <a href="/wiki/Frans_van_Schooten" title="Frans van Schooten">Frans van Schooten</a>'s expanded second Latin edition of the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 100">: 100 </span></sup> Newton continued Descartes' work on <a href="/wiki/Cubic_equation" title="Cubic equation">cubic equations</a>, which freed the subject from fetters of the Greek perspectives. The most important concept was his very modern treatment of single variables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 109–129">: 109–129 </span></sup>  Newton rejected Descartes' vortex theory of planetary motion in favor of his <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton's law of universal gravitation">law of universal gravitation</a>, and most of the second book of Newton's <a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a> is devoted to his counterargument.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_reception">Contemporary reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Contemporary reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In commercial terms, The <i>Discourse</i> appeared during Descartes's lifetime in a single edition of 500 copies, 200 of which were set aside for the author. Sharing a similar fate was the only French edition of The <i>Meditations</i>, which had not managed to sell out by the time of Descartes's death. A concomitant Latin edition of the latter was, however, eagerly sought out by Europe's scholarly community and proved a commercial success for Descartes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: xliii–xliv">: xliii–xliv </span></sup>
</p><p>Although Descartes was well known in academic circles towards the end of his life, the teaching of his works in schools was controversial. Henri de Roy (<a href="/wiki/Henricus_Regius" title="Henricus Regius">Henricus Regius</a>, 1598–1679), Professor of Medicine at the University of Utrecht, was condemned by the Rector of the university, <a href="/wiki/Gisbertus_Voetius" title="Gisbertus Voetius">Gijsbert Voet</a> (Voetius), for teaching Descartes's physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Purported_Rosicrucianism">Purported Rosicrucianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Purported Rosicrucianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The membership of Descartes to the <a href="/wiki/Rosicrucianism" title="Rosicrucianism">Rosicrucians</a> is debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>The initials of his name have been linked to the R.C. <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronym</a> widely used by Rosicrucians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi_10.4000_miranda.1939_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi_10.4000_miranda.1939-158">[150]</a></sup> Furthermore, in 1619 Descartes moved to <a href="/wiki/Ulm" title="Ulm">Ulm</a> which was a well renowned international center of the Rosicrucian movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi_10.4000_miranda.1939_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi_10.4000_miranda.1939-158">[150]</a></sup>
During his journey in Germany, Descartes met Johannes Faulhaber who had previously expressed his personal commitment to join the brotherhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes dedicated the work titled <i>The Mathematical Treasure Trove of Polybius, Citizen of the World</i> to "learned men throughout the world 
and especially to the distinguished B.R.C. (Brothers  of the Rosy Cross) in Germany". The work wasn't completed and its publication is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1618. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=_KzBJf8q37wC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;source=gbs_ge_summary_r&amp;cad=0#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Musicae Compendium</i></a>. A treatise on music theory and the aesthetics of music, which Descartes dedicated to early collaborator Isaac Beeckman (written in 1618, first published—posthumously—in 1650).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 127–129">: 127–129 </span></sup></li>
<li>1626–1628. <i>Regulae ad directionem ingenii</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Rules_for_the_Direction_of_the_Mind" title="Rules for the Direction of the Mind">Rules for the Direction of the Mind</a></i>). Incomplete. First published posthumously in Dutch translation in 1684 and in the original Latin at Amsterdam in 1701 (<i>R. Des-Cartes Opuscula Posthuma Physica et Mathematica</i>). The best critical edition, which includes the Dutch translation of 1684, is edited by Giovanni Crapulli (The Hague: Martinus Nijhoff, 1966).</li>
<li>c. 1630. <i>De solidorum elementis</i>. Concerns the classification of <a href="/wiki/Platonic_solid" title="Platonic solid">Platonic solids</a> and three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Figurate_number" title="Figurate number">figurate numbers</a>. Said by some scholars to prefigure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler%27s_polyhedral_formula" title="Euler's polyhedral formula">Euler's polyhedral formula</a>. Unpublished; discovered in Descartes' estate in Stockholm 1650, soaked for three days in the Seine in a shipwreck while being shipped back to Paris, copied in 1676 by Leibniz, and lost. Leibniz's copy, also lost, was rediscovered circa 1860 in Hannover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[154]</a></sup></li>
<li>1630–1631. <i>La recherche de la vérité par la lumière naturelle</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Search_for_Truth_by_Natural_Light" title="The Search for Truth by Natural Light">The Search for Truth by Natural Light</a></i>) unfinished dialogue published in 1701.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 264ff">: 264ff </span></sup></li>
<li>1630–1633. <i>Le Monde</i> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_World_(Descartes)" title="The World (Descartes)">The World</a></i>) and <i>L'Homme</i> (<i>Man</i>). Descartes's first systematic presentation of his natural philosophy. <i>Man</i> was published posthumously in Latin translation in 1662; and <i>The World</i> posthumously in 1664.</li>
<li>1637. <i>Discours de la méthode</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">Discourse on the Method</a></i>). An introduction to the <i>Essais</i>, which include the <i>Dioptrique</i>, the <i>Météores</i> and the <i>Géométrie</i>.</li>
<li>1637. <i><a href="/wiki/La_G%C3%A9om%C3%A9trie" title="La Géométrie">La Géométrie</a></i> (<i>Geometry</i>). Descartes's major work in mathematics. There is an English translation by Michael Mahoney (New York: Dover, 1979).</li>
<li>1641. <i>Meditationes de prima philosophia</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Meditations_on_First_Philosophy" title="Meditations on First Philosophy">Meditations on First Philosophy</a></i>), also known as <i>Metaphysical Meditations</i>. In Latin; a second edition, published the following year, included an additional objection and reply, and a <i>Letter to Dinet</i>. A French translation by the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Luynes" title="Duke of Luynes">Duke of Luynes</a>, probably done without Descartes's supervision, was published in 1647. Includes six <a href="/wiki/Meditations_on_First_Philosophy#Objections_and_replies" title="Meditations on First Philosophy">Objections and Replies</a>.</li>
<li>1644. <i>Principia philosophiae</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i>), a Latin textbook at first intended by Descartes to replace the Aristotelian textbooks then used in universities. A French translation, <i>Principes de philosophie</i> by Claude Picot, under the supervision of Descartes, appeared in 1647 with a letter-preface to Princess Elisabeth of Bohemia.</li>
<li>1647. <i>Notae in programma</i> (<i>Comments on a Certain Broadsheet</i>). A reply to Descartes's one-time disciple Henricus Regius.</li>
<li>1648. <i>La description du corps humain</i> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Description_of_the_Human_Body" title="The Description of the Human Body">The Description of the Human Body</a></i>). Published posthumously by Clerselier in 1667.</li>
<li>1648. <i>Responsiones Renati Des Cartes...</i> (<i>Conversation with Burman</i>). Notes on a Q&amp;A session between Descartes and Frans Burman on 16 April 1648. Rediscovered in 1895 and published for the first time in 1896. An annotated bilingual edition (Latin with French translation), edited by Jean-Marie Beyssade, was published in 1981 (Paris: PUF).</li>
<li>1649. <i>Les passions de l'âme</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Passions_of_the_Soul" title="Passions of the Soul">Passions of the Soul</a></i>). Dedicated to Princess <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_of_the_Palatinate" title="Elisabeth of the Palatinate">Elisabeth of the Palatinate</a>.</li>
<li>1657. <i>Correspondance</i> (three volumes: 1657, 1659, 1667). Published by Descartes's literary executor <a href="/wiki/Claude_Clerselier" title="Claude Clerselier">Claude Clerselier</a>. The third edition, in 1667, was the most complete; Clerselier omitted, however, much of the material pertaining to mathematics.</li></ul>
<p>In January 2010, a previously unknown letter from Descartes, dated 27 May 1641, was found by the Dutch philosopher Erik-Jan Bos when browsing through <a href="/wiki/Google_Search" title="Google Search">Google</a>. Bos found the letter mentioned in a summary of autographs kept by <a href="/wiki/Haverford_College" title="Haverford College">Haverford College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Haverford,_Pennsylvania" title="Haverford, Pennsylvania">Haverford, Pennsylvania</a>. The college was unaware that the letter had never been published. This was the third letter by Descartes found in the last 25 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 195px"><div style="width: 195px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collected_editions">Collected editions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Collected editions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul><li><i>Oeuvres de Descartes</i> edited by Charles Adam and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tannery" title="Paul Tannery">Paul Tannery</a>, Paris: Léopold Cerf, 1897–1913, 13 volumes; new revised edition, Paris: Vrin-CNRS, 1964–1974, 11 volumes (the first 5 volumes contains the correspondence). [This edition is traditionally cited with the initials <i>AT</i> (for Adam and Tannery) followed by a volume number in Roman numerals; thus <i>AT</i> VII refers to <i>Oeuvres de Descartes</i> volume 7.]</li>
<li><i>Étude du bon sens, La recherche de la vérité et autres écrits de jeunesse (1616–1631)</i> edited by Vincent Carraud and Gilles Olivo, Paris: PUF, 2013.</li>

<li>III: <i>Discours de la Méthode et Essais</i>, 2009.</li>
<li>VIII.1: <i>Correspondance, 1</i> edited by Jean-Robert Armogathe, 2013.</li>
<li>VIII.2: <i>Correspondance, 2</i> edited by Jean-Robert Armogathe, 2013.</li></ul></li>
<li><i>René Descartes. Opere 1637–1649</i>, Milano, Bompiani, 2009, pp. 2531. Edizione integrale (di prime edizioni) e traduzione italiana a fronte, a cura di G. Belgioioso con la collaborazione di I. Agostini, M. Marrone, M. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_editions_of_specific_works">Early editions of specific works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Early editions of specific works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1054258005" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/philsci/id/14760" rel="nofollow"><i>Discours de la methode</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304101305/http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/philsci/id/14760" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 4 March 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, 1637</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/earththeory/id/49760" rel="nofollow"><i>Renati Des-Cartes Principia philosophiæ</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160409041221/http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/earththeory/id/49760" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 9 April 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, 1644</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/earththeory/id/42574" rel="nofollow"><i>Le monde de Mr. Descartes ou le traité de la lumiere</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304192036/http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/earththeory/id/42574" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 4 March 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, 1664</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/math/id/13704" rel="nofollow"><i>Geometria</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20201024091758/http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/math/id/13704" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 24 October 2020 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, 1659</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/philsci/id/31030" rel="nofollow"><i>Meditationes de prima philosophia</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20201024092845/http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/philsci/id/31030" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 24 October 2020 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, 1670</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/philsci/id/33894" rel="nofollow"><i>Opera philosophica</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20201027015852/http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/ref/collection/philsci/id/33894" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 27 October 2020 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, 1672</li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collected_English_translations">Collected English translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Collected English translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1054258005" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul><li>1955. <i>The Philosophical Works</i>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Haldane" title="Elizabeth Haldane">E.S. Haldane</a> and G.R.T. Ross, trans. Dover Publications. This work is traditionally cited with the initials <i>HR</i> (for Haldane and Ross) followed by a volume number in Roman numerals; thus <i>HR</i> II refers to volume 2 of this edition.</li>
<li>1988. <i>The Philosophical Writings of Descartes</i> in 3 vols. <a href="/wiki/John_Cottingham" title="John Cottingham">Cottingham, J.</a>, Stoothoff, R., <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kenny" title="Anthony Kenny">Kenny, A.</a>, and Murdoch, D., trans. Cambridge University Press. This work is traditionally cited with the initials <i>CSM</i> (for Cottingham, Stoothoff, and Murdoch) or <i>CSMK</i> (for Cottingham, Stoothoff, Murdoch, and Kenny) followed by a volume number in Roman numeral; thus <i>CSM</i> II refers to volume 2 of this edition.</li>
<li>1998. <i>René Descartes: The World and Other Writings.</i> Translated and edited by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gaukroger" title="Stephen Gaukroger">Stephen Gaukroger</a>. Cambridge University Press. (This consists mainly of scientific writings, on physics, biology, astronomy, optics, etc., which were very influential in the 17th and 18th centuries, but which are routinely omitted or much abridged in modern collections of Descartes's <i>philosophical</i> works.)</li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Translation_of_single_works">Translation of single works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Translation of single works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1054258005" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul><li>1628. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=fZvuT6Q76GAC&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow"><i>Regulae ad directionem ingenii. Rules for the Direction of the Natural Intelligence. A Bilingual Edition of the Cartesian Treatise on Method</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210816214243/https://books.google.com/books?id=fZvuT6Q76GAC&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 16 August 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, ed. &amp; trans. G. Heffernan (Amsterdam/Atlanta: Rodopi, 1998).</li>
<li>1633. <a class="external text" href="http://www.princeton.edu/~hos/mike/texts/descartes/world/worldfr.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>The World, or Treatise on Light</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210621095514/http://www.princeton.edu/~hos/mike/texts/descartes/world/worldfr.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 21 June 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, tr. by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_S._Mahoney" title="Michael S. Mahoney">Michael S. Mahoney</a>.</li>
<li>1633. <i>Treatise of Man</i>, tr. by T. S. Hall. Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1972.</li>
<li>1637. <i>Discourse on the Method, Optics, Geometry and Meteorology</i>, trans. <a href="/wiki/Paul_J._Olscamp" title="Paul J. Olscamp">P. J. Olscamp</a>, Revised edition (Indianapolis: <a href="/wiki/Hackett_Publishing_Company" title="Hackett Publishing Company">Hackett</a>, 2001).</li>
<li>1637. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=TXopSMll0tEC&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow"><i>The Geometry of René Descartes</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210816214242/https://books.google.com/books?id=TXopSMll0tEC&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 16 August 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, trans. D. E. Smith &amp; Marcia Latham (Chicago: <a href="/wiki/Open_Court_Publishing_Company" title="Open Court Publishing Company">Open Court</a>, 1925).</li>
<li>1641. <i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i>, tr. by J. Cottingham, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1996. Latin original. Alternative English title: <i>Metaphysical Meditations</i>. Includes six <i>Objections and Replies</i>. A second edition published the following year, includes an additional <i>Objection and Reply</i> and a <i>Letter to Dinet</i>. <a class="external text" href="http://www.wright.edu/cola/descartes/intro.html" rel="nofollow">HTML Online Latin-French-English Edition</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060827231748/http://www.wright.edu/cola/descartes/intro.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 27 August 2006 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.</li>
<li>1644. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=2KNFBgAAQBAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow"><i>Principles of Philosophy</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200930220348/https://books.google.com/books?id=2KNFBgAAQBAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 30 September 2020 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, trans. V. R. Miller &amp; R. P. Miller: (<a href="/wiki/Dordrecht" title="Dordrecht">Dordrecht</a>/Boston/London: <a href="/wiki/Springer_Science%2BBusiness_Media#History" title="Springer Science+Business Media">Kluwer Academic Publishers</a>, 1982).</li>
<li>1648. <i>Descartes' Conversation with Burman</i>, tr. by J. Cottingham, Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1989.</li>
<li>1649. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Ca9aBAAAQBAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow"><i>Passions of the Soul</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210816214249/https://books.google.com/books?id=Ca9aBAAAQBAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 16 August 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, trans. S. H. Voss (Indianapolis: Hackett, 1989). Dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_of_the_Palatinate" title="Elisabeth of the Palatinate">Elisabeth of the Palatinate</a>.</li>
<li>1619–1648. <i>René Descartes, Isaac Beeckman, Marin Mersenne. Lettere 1619–1648</i>, ed. by Giulia Beglioioso and Jean Robert-Armogathe, Milano, Bompiani, 2015 pp. 1696. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-88-452-8071-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-88-452-8071-9">978-88-452-8071-9</a></li></ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the uncertain authorship of this most iconic portrait of Descartes was traditionally attributed to Frans Hals, there is no record of their meeting. During the 20th century the assumption was widely challenged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Gilson-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Gilson_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Gilson_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Gilson" title="Étienne Gilson">Étienne Gilson</a> argued in <i>La Liberté chez Descartes et la Théologie</i> (Alcan, 1913, pp. 132–147) that Duns Scotus was not the source of Descartes's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_theological_voluntarism" title="Medieval theological voluntarism">Voluntarism</a>. Although there exist doctrinal differences between Descartes and Scotus "it is still possible to view Descartes as borrowing from a Scotist Voluntarist tradition".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">Adjectival form</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesian</a></i> <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="/ʒ/: 's' in 'pleasure'">ʒ</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This idea had already been proposed by <a href="/wiki/Spanish_philosophy" title="Spanish philosophy">Spanish philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B3mez_Pereira" title="Gómez Pereira">Gómez Pereira</a> a hundred years ago in the form: "I know that I know something, anyone who knows exists, then I exist" (<i>nosco me aliquid noscere, &amp; quidquid noscit, est, ergo ego sum</i>). <div><ul><li>Pereira, Gómez. 1749 [1554]. "De Immortalitate Animae." <i>Antoniana Margarita</i>. p. 277.</li><li>Santos López, Modesto. 1986. "Gómez Pereira, médico y filósofo medinense." In <i>Historia de Medina del Campo y su Tierra, volumen I: Nacimiento y expansión</i>, edited by E. L. Sanz.</li></ul></div></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See also: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/epistemological_turn" title="wiktionary:epistemological turn">Epistemological turn</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While in the Netherlands he changed his address frequently, living among other places in Dordrecht (1628), <a href="/wiki/Franeker" title="Franeker">Franeker</a> (1629), Amsterdam (1629–1630), <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a> (1630), Amsterdam (1630–1632), Deventer (1632–1634), Amsterdam (1634–1635), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utrecht_(city)" title="Utrecht (city)">Utrecht</a> (1635–1636), Leiden (1636), <a href="/wiki/Egmond_aan_den_Hoef" title="Egmond aan den Hoef">Egmond</a> (1636–1638), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santpoort" title="Santpoort">Santpoort</a> (1638–1640), Leiden (1640–1641), Endegeest (a castle near <a href="/wiki/Oegstgeest" title="Oegstgeest">Oegstgeest</a>) (1641–1643), and finally for an extended time in <a href="/wiki/Egmond-Binnen" title="Egmond-Binnen">Egmond-Binnen</a> (1643–1649).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He had lived with <a href="/wiki/Henricus_Reneri" title="Henricus Reneri">Henricus Reneri</a> in Deventer and Amsterdam, and had met with <a href="/wiki/Constantijn_Huygens" title="Constantijn Huygens">Constantijn Huygens</a> and Vopiscus Fortunatus Plempius; Descartes was interviewed by Frans Burman at Egmond-Binnen in 1648. <a href="/wiki/Henricus_Regius" title="Henricus Regius">Henricus Regius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Jansz_de_Jonge_Stampioen" title="Jan Jansz de Jonge Stampioen">Jan Stampioen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frans_van_Schooten" title="Frans van Schooten">Frans van Schooten</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comenius" title="Comenius">Comenius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gisbertus_Voetius" title="Gisbertus Voetius">Gisbertus Voetius</a> were his main opponents.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The remains are, two centuries later, still resting between two other graves—those of the scholarly monks <a href="/wiki/Jean_Mabillon" title="Jean Mabillon">Jean Mabillon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernard_de_Montfaucon" title="Bernard de Montfaucon">Bernard de Montfaucon</a>—in a chapel of the abbey.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Heidegger [1938] (2002), p. 76 quotation: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Descartes... that which he himself founded... modern (and that means, at the same time, Western) metaphysics.</p></blockquote></span>

<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Tad_Schmaltz" title="Tad Schmaltz">Schmaltz, Tad M.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=pIYcUBCOrNgC&amp;pg=PA27" rel="nofollow"><i>Radical Cartesianism: The French Reception of Descartes</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210816214240/https://books.google.com/books?id=pIYcUBCOrNgC&amp;pg=PA27" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 16 August 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> p. 27 quotation: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Descartes most familiar to twentieth-century philosophers is the Descartes of the first two <i>Meditations</i>, someone proccupied with hyperbolic doubt of the material world and the certainty of knowledge of the self that emerges from the famous cogito argument.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Roy_Wood_Sellars" title="Roy Wood Sellars">Roy Wood Sellars</a> (1949) <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/philosophyforfut00sell" rel="nofollow"><i>Philosophy for the future: the quest of modern materialism</i></a> quotation: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Husserl has taken Descartes very seriously in a historical as well as in a systematic sense [...] [in <i>The Crisis of the European Sciences and Transcendental Phenomenology</i>, Husserl] finds in the first two Meditations of Descartes a depth which it is difficult to fathom, and which Descartes himself was so little able to appreciate that he let go "the great discovery" he had in his hands.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Heidegger1938emancipated-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Heidegger1938emancipated_133-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a> [1938] (2002) <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_the_World_Picture" title="The Age of the World Picture">The Age of the World Picture</a></i> quotation: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For up to Descartes...a particular <i>sub-iectum</i>...lies at the foundation of its own fixed qualities and changing circumstances. The superiority of a <i>sub-iectum</i>...arises out of the claim of man to a...self-supported, unshakeable foundation of truth, in the sense of certainty. Why and how does this claim acquire its decisive authority? The claim originates in that emancipation of man in which he frees himself from obligation to Christian revelational truth and Church doctrine to a legislating for himself that takes its stand upon itself.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Edmund_Husserl" title="Edmund Husserl">Husserl, Edmund</a> (1931) <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_Meditations:_An_Introduction_to_Phenomenology" title="Cartesian Meditations: An Introduction to Phenomenology">Cartesian Meditations: An Introduction to Phenomenology</a></i> quotation: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When, with the beginning of modern times, religious belief was becoming more and more externalized as a lifeless convention, men of intellect were lifted by a new belief: their great belief in an autonomous philosophy and science. [...] in philosophy, the <i>Meditations</i> were epoch-making in a quite unique sense, and precisely because of their going back to the pure <i>ego cogito</i>. Descartes work has been used, in fact to inaugurates an entirely new kind of philosophy. Changing its total style, philosophy takes a radical turn: from naïve objectivism to transcendental subjectivism.</p></blockquote></span>

<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">René Descartes, <i>Discourse de la Méthode</i> (Leiden, Netherlands): Jan Maire, 1637, appended book: <i>La Géométrie</i>, book one, <a class="external text" href="http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/btv1b86069594/f383.image" rel="nofollow">p. 299.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20171008182014/http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/btv1b86069594/f383.image" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 8 October 2017 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> From p. 299: <i>" ... "</i> ( ... and <i>aa</i>, or <i>a</i><sup>2</sup>, in order to multiply <i>a</i> by itself; and <i>a</i><sup>3</sup>, in order to multiply it once more by <i>a</i>, and thus to infinity; ... )</span>
